# Typing-Speed-Test

### Typing Speed Test Made with Python Tkinter

[Online Version](https://replit.com/@IrtizaBabar/Typing-Speed-Test) (Keep in mind that it might be very buggy)

**Python** is a [high-level](/wiki/High-level_programming_language "High-level programming language"), [general-purpose programming language](/wiki/General-purpose_programming_language "General-purpose programming language"). Its design philosophy emphasizes [code readability](/wiki/Code_readability "Code readability") with the use of [significant indentation](/wiki/Off-side_rule "Off-side rule"). Its [language constructs](/wiki/Language_construct "Language construct") and [object-oriented](/wiki/Object-oriented_programming "Object-oriented programming") approach aim to help [programmers](/wiki/Programmers "Programmers") write clear, logical code for small- and large-scale projects.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-7_30-0" class="reference">[[30]](#cite_note-AutoNT-7-30)</sup>

Python is [dynamically-typed](/wiki/Type_system#DYNAMIC "Type system") and [garbage-collected](/wiki/Garbage_collection_(computer_science) "Garbage collection (computer science)"). It supports multiple [programming paradigms](/wiki/Programming_paradigm "Programming paradigm"), including [structured](/wiki/Structured_programming "Structured programming") (particularly [procedural](/wiki/Procedural_programming "Procedural programming")), object-oriented and [functional programming](/wiki/Functional_programming "Functional programming"). It is often described as a "batteries included" language due to its comprehensive [standard library](/wiki/Standard_library "Standard library").<sup id="cite_ref-About_31-0" class="reference">[[31]](#cite_note-About-31)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference">[[32]](#cite_note-32)</sup>

[Guido van Rossum](/wiki/Guido_van_Rossum "Guido van Rossum") began working on Python in the late 1980s as a successor to the [ABC programming language](/wiki/ABC_(programming_language) "ABC (programming language)") and first released it in 1991 as Python 0.9.0.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference">[[33]](#cite_note-33)</sup> Python 2.0 was released in 2000 and introduced new features such as [list comprehensions](/wiki/List_comprehension "List comprehension"), [cycle-detecting](/wiki/Cycle_detection "Cycle detection") garbage collection, [reference counting](/wiki/Reference_counting "Reference counting"), and [Unicode](/wiki/Unicode "Unicode") support. Python 3.0, released in 2008, was a major revision that is not completely [backward-compatible](/wiki/Backward_compatibility "Backward compatibility") with earlier versions. Python 2 was discontinued with version 2.7.18 in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference">[[34]](#cite_note-34)</sup>

Python consistently ranks as one of the most popular programming languages.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference">[[35]](#cite_note-35)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference">[[36]](#cite_note-36)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference">[[37]](#cite_note-37)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference">[[38]](#cite_note-38)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: History")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

<div class="thumb tright">

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Guido_van_Rossum_OSCON_2006_cropped.png/150px-Guido_van_Rossum_OSCON_2006_cropped.png)](/wiki/File:Guido_van_Rossum_OSCON_2006_cropped.png)

<div class="thumbcaption">

<div class="magnify">[](/wiki/File:Guido_van_Rossum_OSCON_2006_cropped.png "Enlarge")</div>

The designer of Python, [Guido van Rossum](/wiki/Guido_van_Rossum "Guido van Rossum"), at [OSCON](/wiki/O%27Reilly_Open_Source_Convention "O'Reilly Open Source Convention") 2006</div>

</div>

</div>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style>

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: [History of Python](/wiki/History_of_Python "History of Python")</div>

Python was conceived in the late 1980s<sup id="cite_ref-venners-interview-pt-1_39-0" class="reference">[[39]](#cite_note-venners-interview-pt-1-39)</sup> by [Guido van Rossum](/wiki/Guido_van_Rossum "Guido van Rossum") at [Centrum Wiskunde & Informatica](/wiki/Centrum_Wiskunde_%26_Informatica "Centrum Wiskunde & Informatica") (CWI) in the [Netherlands](/wiki/Netherlands "Netherlands") as a successor to the [ABC programming language](/wiki/ABC_(programming_language) "ABC (programming language)"), which was inspired by [SETL](/wiki/SETL "SETL"),<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-12_40-0" class="reference">[[40]](#cite_note-AutoNT-12-40)</sup> capable of [exception handling](/wiki/Exception_handling "Exception handling") and interfacing with the [Amoeba](/wiki/Amoeba_(operating_system) "Amoeba (operating system)") operating system.<sup id="cite_ref-faq-created_11-1" class="reference">[[11]](#cite_note-faq-created-11)</sup> Its implementation began in December 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline-of-python_41-0" class="reference">[[41]](#cite_note-timeline-of-python-41)</sup> Van Rossum shouldered sole responsibility for the project, as the lead developer, until 12 July 2018, when he announced his "permanent vacation" from his responsibilities as Python's "[benevolent dictator for life](/wiki/Benevolent_dictator_for_life "Benevolent dictator for life")", a title the Python community bestowed upon him to reflect his long-term commitment as the project's chief decision-maker.<sup id="cite_ref-lj-bdfl-resignation_42-0" class="reference">[[42]](#cite_note-lj-bdfl-resignation-42)</sup> In January 2019, active Python core developers elected a five-member Steering Council to lead the project.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference">[[43]](#cite_note-43)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference">[[44]](#cite_note-44)</sup>

Python 2.0 was released on 16 October 2000, with many major new features.<sup id="cite_ref-newin-2.0_45-0" class="reference">[[45]](#cite_note-newin-2.0-45)</sup> Python 3.0, released on 3 December 2008, with many of its major features [backported](/wiki/Backporting "Backporting") to Python 2.6.x<sup id="cite_ref-pep-3000_46-0" class="reference">[[46]](#cite_note-pep-3000-46)</sup> and 2.7.x. Releases of Python 3 include the `2to3` utility, which automates the translation of Python 2 code to Python 3.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference">[[47]](#cite_note-47)</sup>

Python 2.7's [end-of-life](/wiki/End-of-life_(product) "End-of-life (product)") was initially set for 2015, then postponed to 2020 out of concern that a large body of existing code could not easily be forward-ported to Python 3.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference">[[48]](#cite_note-48)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference">[[49]](#cite_note-49)</sup> No further security patches or other improvements will be released for it.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference">[[50]](#cite_note-50)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference">[[51]](#cite_note-51)</sup> With Python 2's [end-of-life](/wiki/End-of-life_(product) "End-of-life (product)"), only Python 3.6.x<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference">[[52]](#cite_note-52)</sup> and later are supported.

Python 3.9.2 and 3.8.8 were expedited<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference">[[53]](#cite_note-53)</sup> as all versions of Python (including 2.7<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference">[[54]](#cite_note-54)</sup>) had security issues leading to possible [remote code execution](/wiki/Remote_code_execution "Remote code execution")<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference">[[55]](#cite_note-55)</sup> and [web cache poisoning](/wiki/Cache_poisoning "Cache poisoning").<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference">[[56]](#cite_note-56)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Design_philosophy_and_features">Design philosophy and features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Design philosophy and features")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Python is a [multi-paradigm programming language](/wiki/Multi-paradigm_programming_language "Multi-paradigm programming language"). [Object-oriented programming](/wiki/Object-oriented_programming "Object-oriented programming") and [structured programming](/wiki/Structured_programming "Structured programming") are fully supported, and many of its features support functional programming and [aspect-oriented programming](/wiki/Aspect-oriented_programming "Aspect-oriented programming") (including by [metaprogramming](/wiki/Metaprogramming "Metaprogramming")<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-13_57-0" class="reference">[[57]](#cite_note-AutoNT-13-57)</sup> and [metaobjects](/wiki/Metaobject "Metaobject") [magic methods] ).<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-14_58-0" class="reference">[[58]](#cite_note-AutoNT-14-58)</sup> Many other paradigms are supported via extensions, including [design by contract](/wiki/Design_by_contract "Design by contract")<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-15_59-0" class="reference">[[59]](#cite_note-AutoNT-15-59)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-16_60-0" class="reference">[[60]](#cite_note-AutoNT-16-60)</sup> and [logic programming](/wiki/Logic_programming "Logic programming").<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-17_61-0" class="reference">[[61]](#cite_note-AutoNT-17-61)</sup>

Python uses [dynamic typing](/wiki/Dynamic_typing "Dynamic typing"), and a combination of [reference counting](/wiki/Reference_counting "Reference counting") and a cycle-detecting garbage collector for [memory management](/wiki/Memory_management "Memory management").<sup id="cite_ref-Reference_counting_62-0" class="reference">[[62]](#cite_note-Reference_counting-62)</sup> It uses dynamic [name resolution](/wiki/Name_resolution_(programming_languages) "Name resolution (programming languages)") ([late binding](/wiki/Late_binding "Late binding")), which binds method and variable names during program execution.

Its design offers some support for functional programming in the [Lisp](/wiki/Lisp_(programming_language) "Lisp (programming language)") tradition. It has `filter`,`map`and`reduce` functions; [list comprehensions](/wiki/List_comprehension "List comprehension"), [dictionaries](/wiki/Associative_array "Associative array"), sets, and [generator](/wiki/Generator_(computer_programming) "Generator (computer programming)") expressions.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-59_63-0" class="reference">[[63]](#cite_note-AutoNT-59-63)</sup> The standard library has two modules (`itertools` and `functools`) that implement functional tools borrowed from [Haskell](/wiki/Haskell_(programming_language) "Haskell (programming language)") and [Standard ML](/wiki/Standard_ML "Standard ML").<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-18_64-0" class="reference">[[64]](#cite_note-AutoNT-18-64)</sup>

Its core philosophy is summarized in the document _The [Zen of Python](/wiki/Zen_of_Python "Zen of Python")_ (_PEP 20_), which includes [aphorisms](/wiki/Aphorism "Aphorism") such as:<sup id="cite_ref-PEP20_65-0" class="reference">[[65]](#cite_note-PEP20-65)</sup>

*   Beautiful is better than ugly.
*   Explicit is better than implicit.
*   Simple is better than complex.
*   Complex is better than complicated.
*   Readability counts.

Rather than building all of its functionality into its core, Python was designed to be highly [extensible](/wiki/Extensibility "Extensibility") via modules. This compact modularity has made it particularly popular as a means of adding programmable interfaces to existing applications. Van Rossum's vision of a small core language with a large standard library and easily extensible interpreter stemmed from his frustrations with [ABC](/wiki/ABC_(programming_language) "ABC (programming language)"), which espoused the opposite approach.<sup id="cite_ref-venners-interview-pt-1_39-1" class="reference">[[39]](#cite_note-venners-interview-pt-1-39)</sup>

Python strives for a simpler, less-cluttered syntax and grammar while giving developers a choice in their coding methodology. In contrast to [Perl](/wiki/Perl "Perl")'s "[there is more than one way to do it](/wiki/There_is_more_than_one_way_to_do_it "There is more than one way to do it")" motto, Python embraces a "there should be one—and preferably only one—obvious way to do it" philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-PEP20_65-1" class="reference">[[65]](#cite_note-PEP20-65)</sup> [Alex Martelli](/wiki/Alex_Martelli "Alex Martelli"), a [Fellow](/wiki/Fellow "Fellow") at the [Python Software Foundation](/wiki/Python_Software_Foundation "Python Software Foundation") and Python book author, wrote: "To describe something as 'clever' is _not_ considered a compliment in the Python culture."<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-19_66-0" class="reference">[[66]](#cite_note-AutoNT-19-66)</sup>

Python's developers strive to avoid [premature optimization](/wiki/Premature_optimization "Premature optimization"), and reject patches to non-critical parts of the [CPython](/wiki/CPython "CPython") reference implementation that would offer marginal increases in speed at the cost of clarity.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-20_67-0" class="reference">[[67]](#cite_note-AutoNT-20-67)</sup> When speed is important, a Python programmer can move time-critical functions to extension modules written in languages such as C; or use [PyPy](/wiki/PyPy "PyPy"), a [just-in-time compiler](/wiki/Just-in-time_compilation "Just-in-time compilation"). [Cython](/wiki/Cython "Cython") is also available, which translates a Python script into C and makes direct C-level API calls into the Python interpreter.

Python's developers aim for it to be fun to use. This is reflected in its name—a tribute to the British comedy group [Monty Python](/wiki/Monty_Python "Monty Python")<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-24_68-0" class="reference">[[68]](#cite_note-AutoNT-24-68)</sup>—and in occasionally playful approaches to tutorials and reference materials, such as examples that refer to spam and eggs (a reference to a [Monty Python sketch](/wiki/Spam_(Monty_Python) "Spam (Monty Python)")) instead of the standard [foo and bar](/wiki/Foobar "Foobar").<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference">[[69]](#cite_note-69)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference">[[70]](#cite_note-70)</sup>

A common [neologism](/wiki/Neologism "Neologism") in the Python community is _pythonic_, which has a wide range of meanings related to program style. "Pythonic" code may use Python idioms well, be natural or show fluency in the language, or conform with Python's minimalist philosophy and emphasis on readability. Code that is difficult to understand or reads like a rough transcription from another programming language is called _unpythonic_.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference">[[71]](#cite_note-71)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference">[[72]](#cite_note-72)</sup>

Python users and admirers, especially those considered knowledgeable or experienced, are often referred to as _Pythonistas_.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-27_73-0" class="reference">[[73]](#cite_note-AutoNT-27-73)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-25_74-0" class="reference">[[74]](#cite_note-AutoNT-25-74)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax_and_semantics">Syntax and semantics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Syntax and semantics")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: [Python syntax and semantics](/wiki/Python_syntax_and_semantics "Python syntax and semantics")</div>

Python is meant to be an easily readable language. Its formatting is visually uncluttered, and often uses English keywords where other languages use punctuation. Unlike many other languages, it does not use [curly brackets](/wiki/Curly_bracket_programming_language "Curly bracket programming language") to delimit blocks, and semicolons after statements are allowed but rarely used. It has fewer syntactic exceptions and special cases than [C](/wiki/C_(programming_language) "C (programming language)") or [Pascal](/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language) "Pascal (programming language)").<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-52_75-0" class="reference">[[75]](#cite_note-AutoNT-52-75)</sup>

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Indentation">Indentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Indentation")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: [Python syntax and semantics § Indentation](/wiki/Python_syntax_and_semantics#Indentation "Python syntax and semantics")</div>

Python uses [whitespace](/wiki/Whitespace_character "Whitespace character") indentation, rather than [curly brackets](/wiki/Curly_bracket_programming_language "Curly bracket programming language") or keywords, to delimit [blocks](/wiki/Block_(programming) "Block (programming)"). An increase in indentation comes after certain statements; a decrease in indentation signifies the end of the current block.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-53_76-0" class="reference">[[76]](#cite_note-AutoNT-53-76)</sup> Thus, the program's visual structure accurately represents its semantic structure.<sup id="cite_ref-guttag_77-0" class="reference">[[77]](#cite_note-guttag-77)</sup> This feature is sometimes termed the [off-side rule](/wiki/Off-side_rule "Off-side rule"). Some other languages use indentation this way; but in most, indentation has no semantic meaning. The recommended indent size is four spaces.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference">[[78]](#cite_note-78)</sup>

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Statements_and_control_flow">Statements and control flow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Statements and control flow")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Python's [statements](/wiki/Statement_(computer_science) "Statement (computer science)") include:

*   The [assignment](/wiki/Assignment_(computer_science) "Assignment (computer science)") statement, using a single equals sign `=`
*   The `[if](/wiki/If-then-else "If-then-else")` statement, which conditionally executes a block of code, along with `else` and `elif` (a contraction of else-if)
*   The `[for](/wiki/Foreach#Python "Foreach")` statement, which iterates over an iterable object, capturing each element to a local variable for use by the attached block
*   The `[while](/wiki/While_loop#Python "While loop")` statement, which executes a block of code as long as its condition is true
*   The `[try](/wiki/Exception_handling_syntax#Python "Exception handling syntax")` statement, which allows exceptions raised in its attached code block to be caught and handled by `except` clauses; it also ensures that clean-up code in a `finally` block is always run regardless of how the block exits
*   The `raise` statement, used to raise a specified exception or re-raise a caught exception
*   The `class` statement, which executes a block of code and attaches its local namespace to a [class](/wiki/Class_(computer_science) "Class (computer science)"), for use in object-oriented programming
*   The `def` statement, which defines a [function](/wiki/Function_(computing) "Function (computing)") or [method](/wiki/Method_(computing) "Method (computing)")
*   The `[with](/wiki/Dispose_pattern#Language_constructs "Dispose pattern")` statement, which encloses a code block within a context manager (for example, acquiring a [lock](/wiki/Lock_(computer_science) "Lock (computer science)") before it is run, then releasing the lock; or opening and closing a [file](/wiki/Computer_file "Computer file")), allowing [resource-acquisition-is-initialization](/wiki/Resource_acquisition_is_initialization "Resource acquisition is initialization") (RAII)-like behavior and replacing a common try/finally idiom<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference">[[79]](#cite_note-79)</sup>
*   The `[break](/wiki/Break_statement "Break statement")` statement, which exits a loop
*   The `continue` statement, which skips the current iteration and continues with the next
*   The `del` statement, which removes a variable—deleting the reference from the name to the value, and producing an error if the variable is referred to before it is redefined
*   The `pass` statement, serving as a [NOP](/wiki/NOP_(code) "NOP (code)"), syntactically needed to create an empty code block
*   The `[assert](/wiki/Assertion_(programming) "Assertion (programming)")` statement, used in debugging to check for conditions that should apply
*   The `yield` statement, which returns a value from a [generator](/wiki/Generator_(computer_programming)#Python "Generator (computer programming)") function (and also an operator); used to implement [coroutines](/wiki/Coroutine "Coroutine")
*   The `return` statement, used to return a value from a function
*   The `[import](/wiki/Include_directive "Include directive")` statement, used to import modules whose functions or variables can be used in the current program

The assignment statement (`=`) binds a name as a [reference](/wiki/Pointer_(computer_programming) "Pointer (computer programming)") to a separate, dynamically-allocated [object](/wiki/Object_(computer_science) "Object (computer science)"). Variables may subsequently be rebound at any time to any object. In Python, a variable name is a generic reference holder without a fixed [data type](/wiki/Type_system "Type system"); however, it always refers to _some_ object with a type. This is called [dynamic typing](/wiki/Dynamic_type "Dynamic type")—in contrast to [statically-typed](/wiki/Statically-typed "Statically-typed") languages, where each variable may contain only a value of a certain type.

Python does not support [tail call](/wiki/Tail_call "Tail call") optimization or [first-class continuations](/wiki/First-class_continuations "First-class continuations"), and, according to van Rossum, it never will.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-55_80-0" class="reference">[[80]](#cite_note-AutoNT-55-80)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-56_81-0" class="reference">[[81]](#cite_note-AutoNT-56-81)</sup> However, better support for [coroutine](/wiki/Coroutine "Coroutine")-like functionality is provided by extending Python's [generators](/wiki/Generator_(computer_programming) "Generator (computer programming)").<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-57_82-0" class="reference">[[82]](#cite_note-AutoNT-57-82)</sup> Before 2.5, generators were [lazy](/wiki/Lazy_evaluation "Lazy evaluation") [iterators](/wiki/Iterator "Iterator"); data was passed unidirectionally out of the generator. From Python 2.5 on, it is possible to pass data back into a generator function; and from version 3.3, it can be passed through multiple stack levels.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-58_83-0" class="reference">[[83]](#cite_note-AutoNT-58-83)</sup>

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Expressions">Expressions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: Expressions")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Some Python [expressions](/wiki/Expression_(computer_science) "Expression (computer science)") are similar to those in languages such as C and [Java](/wiki/Java_(programming_language) "Java (programming language)"), while some are not:

*   Addition, subtraction and multiplication are the same, but the behavior of division differs. There are two types of divisions in Python: [floor division](/wiki/Floor_division "Floor division") (or integer division) `//` and floating-point`/`division.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference">[[84]](#cite_note-84)</sup> Python also uses the `**` operator for exponentiation.
*   The `@` infix operator was introduced in Python 3.5\. It is intended to be used by libraries such as [NumPy](/wiki/NumPy "NumPy") for [matrix multiplication](/wiki/Matrix_multiplication "Matrix multiplication").<sup id="cite_ref-PEP465_85-0" class="reference">[[85]](#cite_note-PEP465-85)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Python3.5Changelog_86-0" class="reference">[[86]](#cite_note-Python3.5Changelog-86)</sup>
*   The syntax `:=`, called the "walrus operator", was introduced in Python 3.8\. It assigns values to variables as part of a larger expression.<sup id="cite_ref-Python3.8Changelog_87-0" class="reference">[[87]](#cite_note-Python3.8Changelog-87)</sup>
*   In Python, `==` compares by value, versus Java, which compares numerics by value<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference">[[88]](#cite_note-88)</sup> and objects by reference.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference">[[89]](#cite_note-89)</sup> Python's `is` operator may be used to compare object identities (comparison by reference), and comparisons may be chained—for example, `<span class="n">a</span> <span class="o"><=</span> <span class="n">b</span> <span class="o"><=</span> <span class="n">c</span>`.
*   Python uses `and`, `or`, and `not` as boolean operators rather than the symbolic `&&`, `||`, `!` in Java and C.
*   Python has a type of expression called a _[list comprehension](/wiki/List_comprehension#Python "List comprehension")_, as well as a more general expression called a _[generator](/wiki/Generator_(computer_programming) "Generator (computer programming)") expression_.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-59_63-1" class="reference">[[63]](#cite_note-AutoNT-59-63)</sup>
*   [Anonymous functions](/wiki/Anonymous_function "Anonymous function") are implemented using [lambda expressions](/wiki/Lambda_(programming) "Lambda (programming)"); however, there may be only one expression in each body.
*   Conditional expressions are written as `<span class="n">x</span> <span class="k">if</span> <span class="n">c</span> <span class="k">else</span> <span class="n">y</span>`<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-60_90-0" class="reference">[[90]](#cite_note-AutoNT-60-90)</sup> (different in order of operands from the `[c ? x : y](/wiki/%3F: "?:")` operator common to many other languages).
*   Python makes a distinction between [lists](/wiki/List_(computer_science) "List (computer science)") and [tuples](/wiki/Tuple "Tuple"). Lists are written as `<span class="p">[</span><span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">3</span><span class="p">]</span>`, are mutable, and cannot be used as the keys of dictionaries (dictionary keys must be [immutable](/wiki/Immutable "Immutable") in Python). Tuples, written as `<span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">3</span><span class="p">)</span>`, are immutable and thus can be used as keys of dictionaries, provided all of the tuple's elements are immutable. The `+` operator can be used to concatenate two tuples, which does not directly modify their contents, but produces a new tuple containing the elements of both. Thus, given the variable `t` initially equal to `<span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">3</span><span class="p">)</span>`, executing `<span class="n">t</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="n">t</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">4</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">5</span><span class="p">)</span>` first evaluates `<span class="n">t</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">4</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">5</span><span class="p">)</span>`, which yields `<span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">3</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">4</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">5</span><span class="p">)</span>`, which is then assigned back to `t`—thereby effectively "modifying the contents" of `t` while conforming to the immutable nature of tuple objects. Parentheses are optional for tuples in unambiguous contexts.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference">[[91]](#cite_note-91)</sup>
*   Python features _sequence unpacking_ where multiple expressions, each evaluating to anything that can be assigned (to a variable, writable property, etc.) are associated in an identical manner to that forming tuple literals—and, as a whole, are put on the left-hand side of the equal sign in an assignment statement. The statement expects an _iterable_ object on the right-hand side of the equal sign that produces the same number of values as the provided writable expressions; when iterated through them, it assigns each of the produced values to the corresponding expression on the left.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference">[[92]](#cite_note-92)</sup>
*   Python has a "string format" operator `%` that functions analogously to `[printf](/wiki/Printf_format_string "Printf format string")` format strings in C—e.g. `<span class="s2">"spam=</span><span class="si">%s</span> <span class="s2">eggs=</span><span class="si">%d</span><span class="s2">"</span> <span class="o">%</span> <span class="p">(</span><span class="s2">"blah"</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">)</span>` evaluates to `"spam=blah eggs=2"`. In Python 2.6+ and 3+, this was supplemented by the `format()` method of the `str` class, e.g. `<span class="s2">"spam=</span><span class="si">{0}</span> <span class="s2">eggs=</span><span class="si">{1}</span><span class="s2">"</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">format</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="s2">"blah"</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">)</span>`. Python 3.6 added "f-strings": `<span class="n">blah</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="s2">"blah"</span><span class="p">;</span> <span class="n">eggs</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">;</span> <span class="sa">f</span><span class="s1">'spam=</span><span class="si">{</span><span class="n">blah</span><span class="si">}</span> <span class="s1">eggs=</span><span class="si">{</span><span class="n">eggs</span><span class="si">}</span><span class="s1">'</span>`.<sup id="cite_ref-pep-0498_93-0" class="reference">[[93]](#cite_note-pep-0498-93)</sup>
*   Strings in Python can be [concatenated](/wiki/Concatenation "Concatenation") by "adding" them (with the same operator as for adding integers and floats), e.g. `<span class="s2">"spam"</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="s2">"eggs"</span>` returns `"spameggs"`. If strings contain numbers, they are added as strings rather than integers, e.g. `<span class="s2">"2"</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="s2">"2"</span>` returns `"22"`.
*   Python has various [string literals](/wiki/String_literal "String literal"):
    *   Delimited by **single or double quote marks**. Unlike in [Unix shells](/wiki/Unix_shell "Unix shell"), [Perl](/wiki/Perl "Perl") and Perl-influenced languages, single and double quote marks function identically. Both use the backslash (`\`) as an [escape character](/wiki/Escape_character "Escape character"). [String interpolation](/wiki/String_interpolation "String interpolation") became available in Python 3.6 as "formatted string literals".<sup id="cite_ref-pep-0498_93-1" class="reference">[[93]](#cite_note-pep-0498-93)</sup>
    *   **Triple-quoted** (beginning and ending with three single or double quote marks), which may span multiple lines and function like [here documents](/wiki/Here_document "Here document") in shells, Perl and [Ruby](/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language) "Ruby (programming language)").
    *   [Raw string](/wiki/Raw_string "Raw string") varieties, denoted by prefixing the string literal with `r`. Escape sequences are not interpreted; hence raw strings are useful where literal backslashes are common, such as [regular expressions](/wiki/Regular_expression "Regular expression") and [Windows](/wiki/Microsoft_Windows "Microsoft Windows")-style paths. (Compare "`@`-quoting" in [C#](/wiki/C_Sharp_(programming_language) "C Sharp (programming language)").)
*   Python has [array index](/wiki/Array_index "Array index") and [array slicing](/wiki/Array_slicing "Array slicing") expressions in lists, denoted as `a[key]`, `<span class="n">a</span><span class="p">[</span><span class="n">start</span><span class="p">:</span><span class="n">stop</span><span class="p">]</span>` or `<span class="n">a</span><span class="p">[</span><span class="n">start</span><span class="p">:</span><span class="n">stop</span><span class="p">:</span><span class="n">step</span><span class="p">]</span>`. Indexes are [zero-based](/wiki/Zero-based_numbering "Zero-based numbering"), and negative indexes are relative to the end. Slices take elements from the _start_ index up to, but not including, the _stop_ index. The third slice parameter, called _step_ or _stride_, allows elements to be skipped and reversed. Slice indexes may be omitted—for example `<span class="n">a</span><span class="p">[:]</span>` returns a copy of the entire list. Each element of a slice is a [shallow copy](/wiki/Shallow_copy "Shallow copy").

In Python, a distinction between expressions and statements is rigidly enforced, in contrast to languages such as [Common Lisp](/wiki/Common_Lisp "Common Lisp"), [Scheme](/wiki/Scheme_(programming_language) "Scheme (programming language)"), or [Ruby](/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language) "Ruby (programming language)"). This leads to duplicating some functionality. For example:

*   [List comprehensions](/wiki/List_comprehensions "List comprehensions") vs. `for`-loops
*   [Conditional](/wiki/Conditional_(programming) "Conditional (programming)") expressions vs. `if` blocks
*   The `eval()` vs. `exec()` built-in functions (in Python 2, `exec` is a statement); the former is for expressions, the latter is for statements

Statements cannot be a part of an expression—so list and other comprehensions or [lambda expressions](/wiki/Lambda_(programming) "Lambda (programming)"), all being expressions, cannot contain statements. A particular case is that an assignment statement such as `<span class="n">a</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="mi">1</span>` cannot form part of the conditional expression of a conditional statement. This has the advantage of avoiding a classic C error of mistaking an assignment operator `=` for an equality operator `==` in conditions: `<span class="k">if</span> <span class="w"></span> <span class="p">(</span><span class="n">c</span> <span class="w"></span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="w"></span> <span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="w"></span> <span class="p">{</span> <span class="w"></span> <span class="p">...</span> <span class="w"></span> <span class="p">}</span><span class="w"></span>` is syntactically valid (but probably unintended) C code, but `<span class="k">if</span> <span class="n">c</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">:</span> <span class="o">...</span>` causes a syntax error in Python.

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">Methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: Methods")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

[Methods](/wiki/Method_(programming) "Method (programming)") on objects are [functions](/wiki/Function_(programming) "Function (programming)") attached to the object's class; the syntax `<span class="n">instance</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">method</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">argument</span><span class="p">)</span>` is, for normal methods and functions, [syntactic sugar](/wiki/Syntactic_sugar "Syntactic sugar") for `<span class="n">Class</span><span class="o">.</span><span class="n">method</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">instance</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">argument</span><span class="p">)</span>`. Python methods have an explicit `[self](/wiki/This_(computer_programming) "This (computer programming)")` parameter to access [instance data](/wiki/Instance_data "Instance data"), in contrast to the implicit self (or `this`) in some other object-oriented programming languages (e.g., [C++](/wiki/C%2B%2B "C++"), Java, [Objective-C](/wiki/Objective-C "Objective-C"), [Ruby](/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language) "Ruby (programming language)")).<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-61_94-0" class="reference">[[94]](#cite_note-AutoNT-61-94)</sup> Python also provides methods, often called _dunder methods_ (due to their names beginning and ending with double-underscores), to allow user-defined classes to modify how they are handled by native operations including length, comparison, in [arithmetic operations](/wiki/Arithmetic_operations "Arithmetic operations") and type conversion.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference">[[95]](#cite_note-95)</sup>

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Typing">Typing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: Typing")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

<div class="thumb tright">

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Python_3._The_standard_type_hierarchy.png/220px-Python_3._The_standard_type_hierarchy.png)](/wiki/File:Python_3._The_standard_type_hierarchy.png)

<div class="thumbcaption">

<div class="magnify">[](/wiki/File:Python_3._The_standard_type_hierarchy.png "Enlarge")</div>

The standard type hierarchy in Python 3</div>

</div>

</div>

Python uses [duck typing](/wiki/Duck_typing "Duck typing") and has typed objects but untyped variable names. Type constraints are not checked at [compile time](/wiki/Compile_time "Compile time"); rather, operations on an object may fail, signifying that it is not of a suitable type. Despite being [dynamically-typed](/wiki/Type_system#Dynamic_type_checking_and_runtime_type_information "Type system"), Python is [strongly-typed](/wiki/Strong_and_weak_typing "Strong and weak typing"), forbidding operations that are not well-defined (for example, adding a number to a string) rather than silently attempting to make sense of them.

Python allows programmers to define their own types using [classes](/wiki/Class_(computer_science) "Class (computer science)"), most often used for [object-oriented programming](/wiki/Object-oriented_programming "Object-oriented programming"). New [instances](/wiki/Object_(computer_science) "Object (computer science)") of classes are constructed by calling the class (for example, `<span class="n">SpamClass</span><span class="p">()</span>` or `<span class="n">EggsClass</span><span class="p">()</span>`), and the classes are instances of the [metaclass](/wiki/Metaclass "Metaclass") `type` (itself an instance of itself), allowing metaprogramming and [reflection](/wiki/Reflection_(computer_science) "Reflection (computer science)").

Before version 3.0, Python had two kinds of classes: _old-style_ and _new-style_.<sup id="cite_ref-classy_96-0" class="reference">[[96]](#cite_note-classy-96)</sup> The syntax of both is the same, the difference being whether the class `object` is inherited from, directly or indirectly (all new-style classes inherit from `object` and are instances of `type`). In versions of Python 2 from Python 2.2 onwards, both kinds of classes can be used. Old-style classes were eliminated in Python 3.0.

The long-term plan is to support [gradual typing](/wiki/Gradual_typing "Gradual typing").<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference">[[97]](#cite_note-97)</sup> From Python 3.5 on, the language's syntax allows specifying static types, but they are not checked in the default implementation, [CPython](/wiki/CPython "CPython"). An experimental optional static type-checker, _mypy_, supports compile-time type checking.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference">[[98]](#cite_note-98)</sup>

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Arithmetic_operations">Arithmetic operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Arithmetic operations")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Python has the usual symbols for arithmetic operators (`+`, `-`, `*`, `/`), the floor division operator `//` and the [modulo operation](/wiki/Modulo_operation "Modulo operation") `%` (where the remainder can be negative, e.g. `4 % -3 == -2`). It also has `**` for [exponentiation](/wiki/Exponentiation "Exponentiation"), e.g. `5**3 == 125` and `9**0.5 == 3.0`, and a matrix‑multiplication operator `@` .<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference">[[102]](#cite_note-102)</sup> These operators work like in traditional math; with the same [precedence rules](/wiki/Order_of_operations "Order of operations"), the operators [infix](/wiki/Infix_notation "Infix notation") (`+` and `-` can also be [unary](/wiki/Unary_operation "Unary operation") to represent positive and negative numbers respectively).

The division between integers produces floating-point results. The behavior of division has changed significantly over time:<sup id="cite_ref-pep0238_103-0" class="reference">[[103]](#cite_note-pep0238-103)</sup>

*   Current Python (i.e. since 3.0) changed `/` to always be floating-point division, e.g. `<span class="mi">5</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="mi">2</span> <span class="o">==</span> <span class="mf">2.5</span>`.
*   Python 2.2 changed integer division to round towards negative infinity, e.g. `7/3 == 2` and `-7/3 == -3`. The floor division `//` operator was introduced. So `7//3 == 2`, `-7//3 == -3`, `7.5//3 == 2.0` and `-7.5//3 == -3.0`. Adding `<span class="kn">from</span> <span class="nn">__future__</span> <span class="kn">import</span> <span class="n">division</span>` causes a module to use Python 3.0 rules for division (see above).
*   Python 2.1 and earlier used C's division behavior. The `/` operator is integer division if both operands are integers, and floating-point division otherwise. Integer division rounds towards 0, e.g. `<span class="mi">7</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="mi">3</span> <span class="o">==</span> <span class="mi">2</span>` and `<span class="o">-</span><span class="mi">7</span><span class="o">/</span><span class="mi">3</span> <span class="o">==</span> <span class="o">-</span><span class="mi">2</span>`.

In Python terms, `/` is _true division_ (or simply _division_), and `//` is _floor division._ `/` before version 3.0 is _classic division_.<sup id="cite_ref-pep0238_103-1" class="reference">[[103]](#cite_note-pep0238-103)</sup>

Rounding towards negative infinity, though different from most languages, adds consistency. For instance, it means that the equation `<span class="p">(</span><span class="n">a</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="n">b</span><span class="p">)</span><span class="o">//</span><span class="n">b</span> <span class="o">==</span> <span class="n">a</span><span class="o">//</span><span class="n">b</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="mi">1</span>` is always true. It also means that the equation `<span class="n">b</span><span class="o">*</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">a</span><span class="o">//</span><span class="n">b</span><span class="p">)</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="n">a</span><span class="o">%</span><span class="n">b</span> <span class="o">==</span> <span class="n">a</span>` is valid for both positive and negative values of `a`. However, maintaining the validity of this equation means that while the result of `a%b` is, as expected, in the [half-open interval](/wiki/Half-open_interval "Half-open interval") [0, _b_), where `b` is a positive integer, it has to lie in the interval (_b_, 0] when `b` is negative.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-62_104-0" class="reference">[[104]](#cite_note-AutoNT-62-104)</sup>

Python provides a `round` function for [rounding](/wiki/Rounding "Rounding") a float to the nearest integer. For [tie-breaking](/wiki/Rounding#Tie-breaking "Rounding"), Python 3 uses [round to even](/wiki/Round_to_even "Round to even"): `round(1.5)` and `round(2.5)` both produce `2`.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-64_105-0" class="reference">[[105]](#cite_note-AutoNT-64-105)</sup> Versions before 3 used [round-away-from-zero](/wiki/Rounding#Rounding_away_from_zero "Rounding"): `round(0.5)` is `1.0`, `round(-0.5)` is `−1.0`.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-63_106-0" class="reference">[[106]](#cite_note-AutoNT-63-106)</sup>

Python allows boolean expressions with multiple equality relations in a manner that is consistent with general use in mathematics. For example, the expression `a < b < c` tests whether `a` is less than `b` and `b` is less than `c`.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-65_107-0" class="reference">[[107]](#cite_note-AutoNT-65-107)</sup> C-derived languages interpret this expression differently: in C, the expression would first evaluate `a < b`, resulting in 0 or 1, and that result would then be compared with `c`.<sup id="cite_ref-CPL_108-0" class="reference">[[108]](#cite_note-CPL-108)</sup>

Python uses [arbitrary-precision arithmetic](/wiki/Arbitrary-precision_arithmetic "Arbitrary-precision arithmetic") for all integer operations. The `Decimal` type/class in the `decimal` module provides [decimal floating-point numbers](/wiki/Decimal_floating_point "Decimal floating point") to a pre-defined arbitrary precision and several rounding modes.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference">[[109]](#cite_note-109)</sup> The `Fraction` class in the `fractions` module provides arbitrary precision for [rational numbers](/wiki/Rational_number "Rational number").<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference">[[110]](#cite_note-110)</sup>

Due to Python's extensive mathematics library, and the third-party library [NumPy](/wiki/NumPy "NumPy") that further extends the native capabilities, it is frequently used as a scientific scripting language to aid in problems such as numerical data processing and manipulation.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference">[[111]](#cite_note-111)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference">[[112]](#cite_note-112)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Programming_examples">Programming examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Programming examples")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

[Hello world](/wiki/%22Hello,_World!%22_program ""Hello, World!" program") program:

<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-python mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr">

<pre><span></span><span class="nb">print</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="s1">'Hello, world!'</span><span class="p">)</span>
</pre>

</div>

Program to calculate the [factorial](/wiki/Factorial "Factorial") of a positive integer:

<div class="mw-highlight mw-highlight-lang-python mw-content-ltr mw-highlight-lines" dir="ltr">

<pre><span></span><span class="linenos" data-line="1"></span><span class="n">n</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="nb">int</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="nb">input</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="s1">'Type a number, and its factorial will be printed: '</span><span class="p">))</span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="2"></span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="3"></span><span class="k">if</span> <span class="n">n</span> <span class="o"><</span> <span class="mi">0</span><span class="p">:</span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="4"></span>    <span class="k">raise</span> <span class="ne">ValueError</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="s1">'You must enter a non-negative integer'</span><span class="p">)</span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="5"></span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="6"></span><span class="n">factorial</span> <span class="o">=</span> <span class="mi">1</span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="7"></span><span class="k">for</span> <span class="n">i</span> <span class="ow">in</span> <span class="nb">range</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="mi">2</span><span class="p">,</span> <span class="n">n</span> <span class="o">+</span> <span class="mi">1</span><span class="p">):</span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="8"></span>    <span class="n">factorial</span> <span class="o">*=</span> <span class="n">i</span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="9"></span>
<span class="linenos" data-line="10"></span><span class="nb">print</span><span class="p">(</span><span class="n">factorial</span><span class="p">)</span>
</pre>

</div>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Libraries">Libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Libraries")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Python's large standard library, commonly cited as one of its greatest strengths,<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-86_113-0" class="reference">[[113]](#cite_note-AutoNT-86-113)</sup> provides tools suited to many tasks. For Internet-facing applications, many standard formats and protocols such as [MIME](/wiki/MIME "MIME") and [HTTP](/wiki/Hypertext_Transfer_Protocol "Hypertext Transfer Protocol") are supported. It includes modules for creating [graphical user interfaces](/wiki/Graphical_user_interface "Graphical user interface"), connecting to [relational databases](/wiki/Relational_database "Relational database"), [generating pseudorandom numbers](/wiki/Pseudorandom_number_generator "Pseudorandom number generator"), arithmetic with arbitrary-precision decimals,<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-88_114-0" class="reference">[[114]](#cite_note-AutoNT-88-114)</sup> manipulating [regular expressions](/wiki/Regular_expression "Regular expression"), and [unit testing](/wiki/Unit_testing "Unit testing").

Some parts of the standard library are covered by specifications—for example, the [Web Server Gateway Interface](/wiki/Web_Server_Gateway_Interface "Web Server Gateway Interface") (WSGI) implementation `wsgiref` follows PEP 333<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-89_115-0" class="reference">[[115]](#cite_note-AutoNT-89-115)</sup>—but most are specified by their code, internal documentation, and [test suites](/wiki/Test_suite "Test suite"). However, because most of the standard library is cross-platform Python code, only a few modules need altering or rewriting for variant implementations.

As of September 2021,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit)</sup> the [Python Package Index](/wiki/Python_Package_Index "Python Package Index") (PyPI), the official repository for third-party Python software, contains over 329,000<sup id="cite_ref-Modulecounts_2021_116-0" class="reference">[[116]](#cite_note-Modulecounts_2021-116)</sup> packages with a wide range of functionality, including:

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style>

<div class="div-col" style="column-width: 30em;">

*   [Automation](/wiki/Automation "Automation")
*   [Data analytics](/wiki/Data_analytics "Data analytics")
*   [Databases](/wiki/Databases "Databases")
*   [Documentation](/wiki/Documentation "Documentation")
*   [Graphical user interfaces](/wiki/Graphical_user_interfaces "Graphical user interfaces")
*   [Image processing](/wiki/Image_processing "Image processing")
*   [Machine learning](/wiki/Machine_learning "Machine learning")
*   [Mobile apps](/wiki/Mobile_app "Mobile app")
*   [Multimedia](/wiki/Multimedia "Multimedia")
*   [Computer networking](/wiki/Computer_networking "Computer networking")
*   [Scientific computing](/wiki/Scientific_computing "Scientific computing")
*   [System administration](/wiki/System_administration "System administration")
*   [Test frameworks](/wiki/Test_framework "Test framework")
*   [Text processing](/wiki/Text_processing "Text processing")
*   [Web frameworks](/wiki/Web_framework "Web framework")
*   [Web scraping](/wiki/Web_scraping "Web scraping")

</div>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Development_environments">Development environments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Development environments")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: [Comparison of integrated development environments § Python](/wiki/Comparison_of_integrated_development_environments#Python "Comparison of integrated development environments")</div>

Most Python implementations (including CPython) include a [read–eval–print loop](/wiki/Read%E2%80%93eval%E2%80%93print_loop "Read–eval–print loop") (REPL), permitting them to function as a [command line interpreter](/wiki/Command_line_interpreter "Command line interpreter") for which users enter statements sequentially and receive results immediately.

Python also comes with an [Integrated development environment (IDE)](/wiki/Integrated_development_environment "Integrated development environment") called [IDLE](/wiki/IDLE "IDLE"), which is more beginner-oriented.

Other shells, including [IDLE](/wiki/IDLE "IDLE") and [IPython](/wiki/IPython "IPython"), add further abilities such as improved auto-completion, session state retention and [syntax highlighting](/wiki/Syntax_highlighting "Syntax highlighting").

As well as standard desktop [integrated development environments](/wiki/Integrated_development_environment "Integrated development environment"), there are [Web browser](/wiki/Web_browser "Web browser")-based IDEs, including [SageMath](/wiki/SageMath "SageMath"), for developing science- and math-related programs; [PythonAnywhere](/wiki/PythonAnywhere "PythonAnywhere"), a browser-based IDE and hosting environment; and Canopy IDE, a commercial IDE emphasizing [scientific computing](/wiki/Scientific_computing "Scientific computing").<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference">[[117]](#cite_note-117)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Implementations">Implementations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Implementations")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: [List of Python software § Python implementations](/wiki/List_of_Python_software#Python_implementations "List of Python software")</div>

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Reference_implementation">Reference implementation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Reference implementation")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

[CPython](/wiki/CPython "CPython") is the [reference implementation](/wiki/Reference_implementation "Reference implementation") of Python. It is written in C, meeting the [C89](/wiki/C89_(C_version) "C89 (C version)") standard with several select [C99](/wiki/C99 "C99") features. (With later C versions out, it is considered outdated.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference">[[118]](#cite_note-118)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference">[[119]](#cite_note-119)</sup> CPython includes its own C extensions, but third-party extensions are not limited to older C versions—e.g. they can be implemented with [C11](/wiki/C11_(C_standard_revision) "C11 (C standard revision)") or C++.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference">[[120]](#cite_note-120)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-66_121-0" class="reference">[[121]](#cite_note-AutoNT-66-121)</sup>) It compiles Python programs into an intermediate [bytecode](/wiki/Bytecode "Bytecode")<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-67_122-0" class="reference">[[122]](#cite_note-AutoNT-67-122)</sup> which is then executed by its [virtual machine](/wiki/Virtual_machine "Virtual machine").<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-68_123-0" class="reference">[[123]](#cite_note-AutoNT-68-123)</sup> CPython is distributed with a large standard library written in a mixture of C and native Python, and is available for many platforms, including Windows (starting with Python 3.9, the Python installer deliberately fails to install on [Windows 7](/wiki/Windows_7 "Windows 7") and 8;<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference">[[124]](#cite_note-124)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference">[[125]](#cite_note-125)</sup> [Windows XP](/wiki/Windows_XP "Windows XP") was supported until Python 3.5) and most modern [Unix-like](/wiki/Unix-like "Unix-like") systems, including macOS (and [Apple M1](/wiki/Apple_M1 "Apple M1") Macs, since Python 3.9.1, with experimental installer) and unofficial support for e.g. [VMS](/wiki/OpenVMS "OpenVMS").<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference">[[126]](#cite_note-126)</sup> Platform portability was one of its earliest priorities.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-69_127-0" class="reference">[[127]](#cite_note-AutoNT-69-127)</sup> (During Python 1 and 2 development, even [OS/2](/wiki/OS/2 "OS/2") and [Solaris](/wiki/Solaris_(operating_system) "Solaris (operating system)") were supported,<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference">[[128]](#cite_note-128)</sup> but support has since been dropped for many platforms.)

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_implementations">Other implementations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Other implementations")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

*   [PyPy](/wiki/PyPy "PyPy") is a fast, compliant interpreter of Python 2.7 and 3.8.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-70_129-0" class="reference">[[129]](#cite_note-AutoNT-70-129)</sup> <sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference">[[130]](#cite_note-130)</sup> Its [just-in-time compiler](/wiki/Just-in-time_compilation "Just-in-time compilation") brings a significant speed improvement over CPython but some libraries written in C cannot be used with it.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-71_131-0" class="reference">[[131]](#cite_note-AutoNT-71-131)</sup>
*   [Stackless Python](/wiki/Stackless_Python "Stackless Python") is a significant fork of CPython that implements [microthreads](/wiki/Microthread "Microthread"); it does not use the [call stack](/wiki/Call_stack "Call stack") in the same way, thus allowing massively concurrent programs. PyPy also has a stackless version.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-73_132-0" class="reference">[[132]](#cite_note-AutoNT-73-132)</sup>
*   [MicroPython](/wiki/MicroPython "MicroPython") and [CircuitPython](/wiki/CircuitPython "CircuitPython") are Python 3 variants optimized for [microcontrollers](/wiki/Microcontroller "Microcontroller"), including [Lego Mindstorms EV3](/wiki/Lego_Mindstorms_EV3 "Lego Mindstorms EV3").<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference">[[133]](#cite_note-133)</sup>
*   Pyston is a variant of the Python runtime that uses just-in-time compilation to speed up the execution of Python programs.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference">[[134]](#cite_note-134)</sup>
*   Cinder is a performance-oriented fork of CPython 3.8 that contains a number of optimizations including bytecode inline caching, eager evaluation of coroutines, a method-at-a-time [JIT](/wiki/Just-in-time_compilation "Just-in-time compilation") and an experimental bytecode compiler.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference">[[135]](#cite_note-135)</sup>

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Unsupported_implementations">Unsupported implementations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Unsupported implementations")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Other just-in-time Python compilers have been developed, but are now unsupported:

*   Google began a project named [Unladen Swallow](/wiki/Unladen_Swallow "Unladen Swallow") in 2009, with the aim of speeding up the Python interpreter fivefold by using the [LLVM](/wiki/LLVM "LLVM"), and of improving its multithreading ability to scale to thousands of cores,<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-74_136-0" class="reference">[[136]](#cite_note-AutoNT-74-136)</sup> while ordinary implementations suffer from the [global interpreter lock](/wiki/Global_interpreter_lock "Global interpreter lock").
*   [Psyco](/wiki/Psyco "Psyco") is a discontinued [just-in-time](/wiki/Just-in-time_compilation "Just-in-time compilation") [specializing](/wiki/Run-time_algorithm_specialization "Run-time algorithm specialization") compiler that integrates with CPython and transforms bytecode to machine code at runtime. The emitted code is specialized for certain [data types](/wiki/Data_type "Data type") and is faster than the standard Python code. Psyco does not support Python 2.7 or later.
*   [PyS60](/wiki/PyS60 "PyS60") was a Python 2 interpreter for [Series 60](/wiki/Series_60 "Series 60") mobile phones released by [Nokia](/wiki/Nokia "Nokia") in 2005\. It implemented many of the modules from the standard library and some additional modules for integrating with the [Symbian](/wiki/Symbian "Symbian") operating system. The Nokia [N900](/wiki/N900 "N900") also supports Python with [GTK](/wiki/GTK "GTK") widget libraries, enabling programs to be written and run on the target device.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference">[[137]](#cite_note-137)</sup>

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Cross-compilers_to_other_languages">Cross-compilers to other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Cross-compilers to other languages")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

There are several compilers to high-level [object languages](/wiki/Object_language "Object language"), with either unrestricted Python, a restricted subset of Python, or a language similar to Python as the source language:

*   [Cython](/wiki/Cython "Cython") compiles (a superset of) Python 2.7 to C (while the resulting code is also usable with Python 3 and also e.g. C++).
*   [Nuitka](/wiki/Nuitka "Nuitka") compiles Python into C++.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference">[[138]](#cite_note-138)</sup>
*   Pythran compiles a subset of Python 3 to C++.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference">[[139]](#cite_note-139)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference">[[140]](#cite_note-140)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference">[[141]](#cite_note-141)</sup>
*   [Pyrex](/wiki/Pyrex_(programming_language) "Pyrex (programming language)") (latest release in 2010) and [Shed Skin](/wiki/Shed_Skin "Shed Skin") (latest release in 2013) compile to C and C++ respectively.
*   Google's Grumpy (latest release in 2017) [transpiles](/wiki/Transpile "Transpile") Python 2 to [Go](/wiki/Go_(programming_language) "Go (programming language)").<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference">[[142]](#cite_note-142)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference">[[143]](#cite_note-143)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference">[[144]](#cite_note-144)</sup>
*   [IronPython](/wiki/IronPython "IronPython") allows running Python 2.7 programs (and an [alpha](/wiki/Software_release_life_cycle#Alpha "Software release life cycle"), released in 2021, is also available for "Python 3.4, although features and behaviors from later versions may be included"<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference">[[145]](#cite_note-145)</sup>) on the .NET [Common Language Runtime](/wiki/Common_Language_Runtime "Common Language Runtime").<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference">[[146]](#cite_note-146)</sup>
*   [Jython](/wiki/Jython "Jython") compiles Python 2.7 to Java bytecode, allowing the use of the Java libraries from a Python program.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference">[[147]](#cite_note-147)</sup>
*   [MyHDL](/wiki/MyHDL "MyHDL") is a Python-based [hardware description language](/wiki/Hardware_description_language "Hardware description language") (HDL), that converts MyHDL code to [Verilog](/wiki/Verilog "Verilog") or [VHDL](/wiki/VHDL "VHDL") code.
*   [Numba](/wiki/Numba "Numba") uses LLVM to compile a subset of Python to machine code.
*   Brython,<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference">[[148]](#cite_note-148)</sup> Transcrypt<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference">[[149]](#cite_note-149)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference">[[150]](#cite_note-150)</sup> and [Pyjs](/wiki/Pyjs "Pyjs") (latest release in 2012) compile Python to [JavaScript](/wiki/JavaScript "JavaScript").
*   [RPython](/wiki/RPython "RPython") can be compiled to C, and is used to build the PyPy interpreter of Python.

### <span class="mw-headline" id="Performance">Performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Performance")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

A performance comparison of various Python implementations on a non-numerical (combinatorial) workload was presented at EuroSciPy '13.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference">[[151]](#cite_note-151)</sup> Python's performance compared to other programming languages is also benchmarked by [The Computer Language Benchmarks Game](/wiki/The_Computer_Language_Benchmarks_Game "The Computer Language Benchmarks Game").<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference">[[152]](#cite_note-152)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Development")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Python's development is conducted largely through the _Python Enhancement Proposal_ (PEP) process, the primary mechanism for proposing major new features, collecting community input on issues and documenting Python design decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-PepCite000_153-0" class="reference">[[153]](#cite_note-PepCite000-153)</sup> Python coding style is covered in PEP 8.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference">[[154]](#cite_note-154)</sup> Outstanding PEPs are reviewed and commented on by the Python community and the steering council.<sup id="cite_ref-PepCite000_153-1" class="reference">[[153]](#cite_note-PepCite000-153)</sup>

Enhancement of the language corresponds with development of the CPython reference implementation. The mailing list python-dev is the primary forum for the language's development. Specific issues are discussed in the [Roundup](/wiki/Roundup_(issue_tracker) "Roundup (issue tracker)") [bug tracker](/wiki/Bug_tracker "Bug tracker") hosted at [bugs.python.org](https://bugs.python.org).<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-21_155-0" class="reference">[[155]](#cite_note-AutoNT-21-155)</sup> Development originally took place on a [self-hosted](/wiki/Self-hosting_(web_services) "Self-hosting (web services)") source-code repository running [Mercurial](/wiki/Mercurial "Mercurial"), until Python moved to [GitHub](/wiki/GitHub "GitHub") in January 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-py_dev_guide_156-0" class="reference">[[156]](#cite_note-py_dev_guide-156)</sup>

CPython's public releases come in three types, distinguished by which part of the version number is incremented:

*   Backward-incompatible versions, where code is expected to break and needs to be manually [ported](/wiki/Ported "Ported"). The first part of the version number is incremented. These releases happen infrequently—version 3.0 was released 8 years after 2.0.
*   Major or "feature" releases are largely compatible with the previous version but introduce new features. The second part of the version number is incremented. Starting with Python 3.9, these releases are expected to happen annually.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference">[[157]](#cite_note-157)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference">[[158]](#cite_note-158)</sup> Each major version is supported by bugfixes for several years after its release.<sup id="cite_ref-release-schedule_159-0" class="reference">[[159]](#cite_note-release-schedule-159)</sup>
*   Bugfix releases,<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-22_160-0" class="reference">[[160]](#cite_note-AutoNT-22-160)</sup> which introduce no new features, occur about every 3 months and are made when a sufficient number of bugs have been fixed upstream since the last release. Security vulnerabilities are also patched in these releases. The third and final part of the version number is incremented.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-22_160-1" class="reference">[[160]](#cite_note-AutoNT-22-160)</sup>

Many [alpha, beta, and release-candidates](/wiki/Beta_release "Beta release") are also released as previews and for testing before final releases. Although there is a rough schedule for each release, they are often delayed if the code is not ready. Python's development team monitors the state of the code by running the large [unit test](/wiki/Unit_test "Unit test") suite during development.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-23_161-0" class="reference">[[161]](#cite_note-AutoNT-23-161)</sup>

The major [academic conference](/wiki/Academic_conference "Academic conference") on Python is [PyCon](/wiki/PyCon "PyCon"). There are also special Python mentoring programmes, such as [Pyladies](/wiki/Pyladies "Pyladies").

Python 3.10 deprecated `wstr` (to be removed in Python 3.12; meaning Python extensions<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference">[[162]](#cite_note-162)</sup> need to be modified by then),<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference">[[163]](#cite_note-163)</sup> and added [pattern matching](/wiki/Pattern_matching "Pattern matching") to the language.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference">[[164]](#cite_note-164)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="API_documentation_generators">API documentation generators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: API documentation generators")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Tools that can generate documentation for Python API include [pydoc](/wiki/Pydoc "Pydoc") (available as part of standard library), [Sphinx](/wiki/Sphinx_(documentation_generator) "Sphinx (documentation generator)"), [Pdoc](/wiki/Pdoc "Pdoc") and its forks, [Doxygen](/wiki/Doxygen "Doxygen") and [Graphviz](/wiki/Graphviz "Graphviz"), among others.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference">[[165]](#cite_note-165)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Naming")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Python's name is derived from the British comedy group [Monty Python](/wiki/Monty_Python "Monty Python"), whom Python creator Guido van Rossum enjoyed while developing the language. Monty Python references appear frequently in Python code and culture;<sup id="cite_ref-tutorial-chapter1_166-0" class="reference">[[166]](#cite_note-tutorial-chapter1-166)</sup> for example, the [metasyntactic variables](/wiki/Metasyntactic_variable "Metasyntactic variable") often used in Python literature are [_spam_ and _eggs_](/wiki/Spam_(Monty_Python) "Spam (Monty Python)") instead of the traditional [_foo_ and _bar_](/wiki/Foobar "Foobar").<sup id="cite_ref-tutorial-chapter1_166-1" class="reference">[[166]](#cite_note-tutorial-chapter1-166)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-26_167-0" class="reference">[[167]](#cite_note-AutoNT-26-167)</sup> The official Python documentation also contains various references to Monty Python routines.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference">[[168]](#cite_note-168)</sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference">[[169]](#cite_note-169)</sup>

The prefix _Py-_ is used to show that something is related to Python. Examples of the use of this prefix in names of Python applications or libraries include [Pygame](/wiki/Pygame "Pygame"), a [binding](/wiki/Language_binding "Language binding") of [SDL](/wiki/Simple_DirectMedia_Layer "Simple DirectMedia Layer") to Python (commonly used to create games); [PyQt](/wiki/PyQt "PyQt") and [PyGTK](/wiki/PyGTK "PyGTK"), which bind [Qt](/wiki/Qt_(software) "Qt (software)") and GTK to Python respectively; and [PyPy](/wiki/PyPy "PyPy"), a Python implementation originally written in Python.

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Popularity">Popularity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Popularity")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

Since 2003, Python has consistently ranked in the top ten most popular programming languages in the [TIOBE Programming Community Index](/wiki/TIOBE_Programming_Community_Index "TIOBE Programming Community Index") where, as of October 2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">[[update]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit)</sup>, it is the most popular language (ahead of [Java](/wiki/Java_(programming_language) "Java (programming language)"), and C).<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference">[[170]](#cite_note-170)</sup> It was selected Programming Language of the Year (for "the highest rise in ratings in a year") in 2007, 2010, 2018, and 2020 (the only language to do so four times<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference">[[171]](#cite_note-171)</sup>).<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-34_172-0" class="reference">[[172]](#cite_note-AutoNT-34-172)</sup>

An empirical study found that scripting languages, such as Python, are more productive than conventional languages, such as C and Java, for programming problems involving string manipulation and search in a dictionary, and determined that memory consumption was often "better than Java and not much worse than C or C++".<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-28_173-0" class="reference">[[173]](#cite_note-AutoNT-28-173)</sup>

Large organizations that use Python include [Wikipedia](/wiki/Wikipedia "Wikipedia"), [Google](/wiki/Google "Google"),<sup id="cite_ref-quotes-about-python_174-0" class="reference">[[174]](#cite_note-quotes-about-python-174)</sup> [Yahoo!](/wiki/Yahoo! "Yahoo!"),<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-29_175-0" class="reference">[[175]](#cite_note-AutoNT-29-175)</sup> [CERN](/wiki/CERN "CERN"),<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-30_176-0" class="reference">[[176]](#cite_note-AutoNT-30-176)</sup> [NASA](/wiki/NASA "NASA"),<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-31_177-0" class="reference">[[177]](#cite_note-AutoNT-31-177)</sup> [Facebook](/wiki/Facebook "Facebook"),<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference">[[178]](#cite_note-178)</sup> [Amazon](/wiki/Amazon_(company) "Amazon (company)"), [Instagram](/wiki/Instagram "Instagram"),<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference">[[179]](#cite_note-179)</sup> [Spotify](/wiki/Spotify "Spotify")<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference">[[180]](#cite_note-180)</sup> and some smaller entities like [ILM](/wiki/Industrial_Light_%26_Magic "Industrial Light & Magic")<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-32_181-0" class="reference">[[181]](#cite_note-AutoNT-32-181)</sup> and [ITA](/wiki/ITA_Software "ITA Software").<sup id="cite_ref-AutoNT-33_182-0" class="reference">[[182]](#cite_note-AutoNT-33-182)</sup> The social news networking site [Reddit](/wiki/Reddit "Reddit") was written mostly in Python.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference">[[183]](#cite_note-183)</sup>

## <span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span>[edit](/w/index.php?title=Python_(programming_language)&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Uses")<span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span>

<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096">

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: [List of Python software](/wiki/List_of_Python_software "List of Python software")</div>
